reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "facebook/bart-large"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32
batch_size = 1024

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

Also, for seq_len=32, using a custom kernel that does Q@K.T + causal_mask + softmax + @V all in shared memory could be significantly faster because it avoids writing Q, K, V, attention scores, attention probabilities, and attention output to global memory multiple times. It computes everything in registers/shared memory for small 32x32 tiles. Let me think about this. Each head has: - Q: 32 x 64 - K: 32 x 64 - V: 32 x 64 - Scores: 32 x 32 - Attn_out: 32 x 64 In the standard approach: 1. QKV projections: read x (32*1024), write Q, K, V (3*32*1024) - memory: read 32KB, write 96KB 2. Q@K.T: read Q, K, write scores (32*32*4=4KB) 3. Add mask, softmax: read/write scores 4. Attn@V: read scores, V, write out (32*1024=32KB) 5. Output proj: read out, write result (32*1024 each) Total memory traffic per head: ~300KB For 16*1024 heads per layer: ~4.8GB per layer For 12 layers: ~58GB of memory traffic just for attention! Wait, that can't be right because intermediate activations are kept in global memory between kernel launches. So yes, every tensor is written and read from DRAM. For attention with (bs=1024, num_heads=16, seq=32), the Q tensor is 1024 * 16 * 32 * 64 * 4 bytes = 134MB. Written once in QKV proj, read once in Q@K.T. Similarly for K, V. So attention traffic per layer: - x in QKV proj: ~134MB read, ~402MB write (Q,K,V) - Q@K.T: ~268MB read (Q,K), ~4MB write (scores) - Mask+Softmax: ~4MB read, ~4MB write - Attn@V: ~134MB read (scores, V), ~134MB write (out) - Output proj: ~134MB read, ~134MB write Total per layer: ~134 + 536 + 268 + 134 + 4 + 8 + 134 + 134 = ~1.35GB Times 12 layers = ~16GB of memory traffic. At 2TB/s, reading and writing 16GB takes 8ms. But we're also doing GEMMs (cuBLAS might not be bandwidth-bound if it achieves good compute utilization). If I fuse attention entirely into one kernel: - Read x: 134MB - Compute Q, K, V in registers, immediately compute Q@K.T - Compute softmax - Compute attn@V - Compute output proj - Write final attention output: 134MB Total memory traffic: 268MB per layer, 3.2GB total. That's a 5x reduction in memory traffic for the attention component! For the MLP: - Each layer reads x (134MB), computes FC1 (134MB write for intermediate), GELU (in-place), FC2 (134MB write), add residual. Total per layer: ~536MB read/write. Times 12 = 6.4GB. If I could fuse LN + FC1 + GELU + FC2 + residual into one kernel (with cuBLAS Device or by loading weights to shared memory), I could reduce traffic. But as I said, fusing cuBLAS-size GEMMs is hard. However, fusing attention is VERY practical for seq_len=32! The entire attention computation for a head fits in shared memory or registers. Each head only needs 32x64 floats = 8KB. For a warp of 32 threads, each could handle one or two rows. With seq_len=32, we can have each block handle multiple heads. This is a genuinely great optimization opportunity! I should write: 1. A fused attention kernel for BART-style attention 2. Host C++ code that calls cuBLAS for the linear projections or... actually, I can even do the QKV projection in the same kernel if I pass the weight matrices, but those are 1024*1024*3 = 12MB per layer. That's too big for shared memory. But I can call cublas for the QKV and output projections, and write a custom kernel for the "Q@K.T + mask + softmax + @V" part. Actually, for QKV projections, a single cublas GEMM is optimal. Then a custom kernel that takes Q, K, V and computes the attention output. Then another cublas GEMM for output projection. The custom attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void bart_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ query, const float* __restrict__ key, const float* __restrict__ value, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { // Each block handles one (batch, head) pair int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / num_heads; int h = bh % num_heads; // Base offsets size_t base = ((size_t)b * num_heads + h) * seq_len * head_dim; size_t score_base = ((size_t)b * num_heads + h) * seq_len * seq_len; // Use shared memory for Q, K, V tiles // Actually seq_len=32, head_dim=64, so Q is 32*64=2048 floats=8KB // Scores is 32*32=1024 floats=4KB // We can easily fit one sequence in shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sQ = smem; float* sK = smem + seq_len * head_dim; float* sV = smem + 2 * seq_len * head_dim; float* sScores = smem + 3 * seq_len * head_dim; // Load Q, K, V from global memory // ... // Compute Q @ K.T for this head // ... // Apply causal mask: for i < j, set to -inf // ... // Softmax over row // ... // Compute Out = Scores @ V // ... // Write output // ... } ``` This is definitely doable! And it could provide a real speedup because it eliminates intermediate memory traffic for Q, K, V, scores, and softmax probabilities. The only global memory reads are Q, K, V (which were just written by cublas), and the output write. Wait - but we just wrote Q, K, V from cublas. So reading them is necessary. But the standard implementation also reads Q, K, V into registers, computes scores, writes scores to global, reads them back for softmax, writes probabilities, reads them for attn@V, writes attn output. My custom kernel eliminates all those intermediates. The Q, K, V tensors never leave the GPU caches. On a well-designed kernel, the Q, K, V writes from cublas can hit L2 cache and be read by the attention kernel without going to DRAM. This is a great strategy! hidden_states return (hidden_states, attn_weights, ...) ``` BartAttention: ```python class BartAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): self.embed_dim = config.d_model self.num_heads = config.decoder_attention_heads self.head_dim = self.embed_dim // self.num_heads self.scaling = self.head_dim ** -0.5 self.q_proj = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, self.embed_dim) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, self.embed_dim) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, self.embed_dim) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, self.embed_dim) def _shape(self, tensor, seq_len, bsz): return tensor.view(bsz, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous() def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask): bsz, tgt_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) * self.scaling key_states = self._shape(self.k_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) value_states = self._shape(self.v_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) proj_shape = (bsz * self.num_heads, -1, self.head_dim) query_states = query_states.view(*proj_shape) key_states = key_states.view(*proj_shape) value_states = value_states.view(*proj_shape) src_len = key_states.size(1) = F.dropout(attn_weights, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_probs, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, self.num_heads, tgt_len, self.head_dim) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().reshape(bsz, tgt_len, self.embed_dim) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, attn_weights, None ``` Wait, the shapes! With 32 threads per block (one per seq position), that's too few threads. Let me think... Actually, for a (32, 64) matmul, if we use a block of 32x8 threads = 256 threads: - Each thread computes one or more output elements - Load K and V into shared memory (32*64 = 2048 floats = 8KB each) - Load Q into registers or shared memory - Compute QK^T (32x32) in registers - Apply causal mask - Softmax over 32 elements (can do warp-level reduction) - Compute attn @ V With 256 threads per block and 16384 blocks, total threads = ~4.2M. Shared memory would be ~24KB for K+V+Q. So we can fit ~3 blocks per SM. With 132 SMs, total active blocks = ~396. With 16384 total blocks, that's ~41 waves. This should be fine. But actually, we can be more efficient. Since seq_len=32 is small, each warp can handle one row of the attention. One (batch, head) pair needs 32 warps (one per query). If we put 32 warps in one block (1024 threads), each block handles one (batch, head). For 16384 (batch, head) pairs, we need 16384 blocks. Each block: 1. Loads Q, K, V from global memory to shared memory - Q: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats - K: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats - V: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats - Total: 6144 floats = 24KB 2. Each warp (32 threads) computes dot products of its query row with all 32 key rows - This gives a row of 32 attention scores 3. Causal mask: set positions j > i to -inf 4. Warp-level softmax on 32 elements 5. Each warp computes weighted sum of 32 value rows to get one output row of 64 elements 6. Write output back This is a classic approach and should be quite efficient for small sequence lengths. For BartDecoderLayer, the structure is: - `self_attn_layer_norm` - `self_attn` which contains: - q_proj, k_proj, v_proj (separate linears) - out_proj (linear) - residual add - `encoder_attn` (cross-attention, skipped) - residual add - `final_layer_norm` - fc1 (up-projection) - activation function - fc2 (down-projection) - residual add For inference without encoder states, cross-attention is skipped. Wait, actually for BART, the LM head often shares weights with the input embeddings. Let me check: in `BartForCausalLM`, the `lm_head` is typically a separate linear or tied to embeddings. Actually, HuggingFace's `BartForCausalLM` usually has `model.shared` (embeddings) and `lm_head` which can be tied or not. By default, it's tied (`tie_word_embeddings=True`). So if tied, the LM head weight is the same as the embedding weight. OK, let me now think about how to structure the actual code. First, I need to understand the exact weight names and shapes. In `BartForCausalLM`: - `model.decoder.embed_tokens.weight`: (50265, 1024) - shared embeddings - `model.decoder.embed_positions.weight`: (1024+2, 1024) - positional embeddings - `model.decoder.layers.*`: 12 layers - `self_attn.q_proj.weight`, `.bias`: (1024, 1024) - `self_attn.k_proj.weight`, `.bias`: (1024, 1024) - `self_attn.v_proj.weight`, `.bias`: (1024, 1024) - `self_attn.out_proj.weight`, `.bias`: (1024, 1024) - `self_attn_layer_norm.weight`, `.bias`: (1024,) - `encoder_attn.*` - cross attention, skipped - `encoder_attn_layer_norm.weight`, `.bias`: (1024,) - might be used even if cross-attn skipped? In BartDecoder: 1. `inputs_embeds = self.embed_tokens(input) * self.embed_scale` 2. `pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(input)` 3. `hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds` 4. `hidden_states = self.layernorm_embedding(hidden_states)` 5. `hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, ...)` 6. Loop through decoder layers And at the end of the decoder, there's typically no final layer norm (Bart uses pre-norm in each layer and no final norm). Actually in BART decoder, the layer structure is: - self_attn_layer_norm - self_attn - residual - cross_attn_layer_norm (if cross-attn exists) - cross_attn (if used) - residual - final_layer_norm - fc1 - activation - dropout - fc2 - residual So for causal LM without encoder, the cross-attn part is skipped entirely. Now, for the actual CUDA kernel, I need to be careful about shapes. `at::linear` expects weight as (out_features, in_features) and does x @ weight.T + bias. Let me write the C++ code structure: ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_tokens_weight, torch::Tensor embed_positions_weight, // ... all the layer weights ... ) { auto options = input_ids.options(); int batch_size = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); // Embedding auto hidden = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids); // Positional embedding auto positions = at::arange(seq_len, options.dtype(at::kLong)) + 2; // BART offset positions = positions.unsqueeze(0).expand({batch_size, -1}); auto pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions); hidden = hidden + pos_embeds; // LayerNorm embedding hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, layernorm_embedding_weight, layernorm_embedding_bias, 1e-5); // Causal mask auto causal_mask = at::full({seq_len, seq_len}, -1e9, options); causal_mask = at::triu(causal_mask, 1); // Decoder layers for (int i = 0; i < 12; i++) { // Self-attention auto residual = hidden; hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, self_attn_layer_norm_weights[i], self_attn_layer_norm_biases[i], 1e-5); auto q = at::linear(hidden, q_proj_weights[i], q_proj_biases[i]); auto k = at::linear(hidden, k_proj_weights[i], k_proj_biases[i]); auto v = at::linear(hidden, v_proj_weights[i], v_proj_biases[i]); // Custom attention kernel hidden = custom_attention(q, k, v, causal_mask); hidden = at::linear(hidden, out_proj_weights[i], out_proj_biases[i]); hidden = residual + hidden; // Cross-attention skipped // FFN residual = hidden; hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, final_layer_norm_weights[i], final_layer_norm_biases[i], 1e-5); hidden = at::linear(hidden, fc1_weights[i], fc1_biases[i]); hidden = at::gelu(hidden); hidden = at::linear(hidden, fc2_weights[i], fc2_biases[i]); hidden = residual + hidden; } // LM head auto logits = at::linear(hidden, lm_head_weight); return logits; } ``` Wait, but BART uses `embed_scale` typically set to `sqrt(hidden_size)` = 32.0. And position embeddings offset by 2. Let me make sure I handle these correctly. Also, BART causal LM passes `use_cache=False` typically. And there might be `output_attentions`, `output_hidden_states`, etc., but we don't need those. For my custom attention kernel, I need to handle the Q, K, V tensors and produce the attention output. In BART's self-attention: - Q, K, V shapes: (batch_size, seq_len, hidden_size) - Reshaped to (batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim) - Scores = Q @ K^T / sqrt(head_dim) - Apply causal mask - Softmax - Output = scores @ V - Reshape back to (batch_size, seq_len, hidden_size) For my kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int batch = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int row = threadIdx.x; // 0 to seq_len-1 if (row >= seq_len) return; // Offset to this batch/head const float* q_ptr = q + ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len + row) * head_dim; const float* k_base = k + (batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim; const float* v_base = v + (batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim; float* out_ptr = out + ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len + row) * head_dim; // Compute attention scores for this row float scores[32]; // assuming seq_len <= 32 float max_score = -1e9f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { if (col > row) { scores[col] = -1e9f; } else { const float* k_ptr = k_base + col * head_dim; float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_ptr[d] * k_ptr[d]; } scores[col] = dot / sqrtf((float)head_dim); max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[col]); } } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] = expf(scores[col] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[col]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { val += scores[col] * v_base[col * head_dim + d]; } out_ptr[d] = val; } } ``` Wait, this has a problem: if `seq_len` is a compile-time constant, we can unroll. But if it's passed as a parameter, unrolling is harder. Since seq_len=32 in this problem, I can make it a template parameter or just use `#pragma unroll` with a constant. Actually, the input `seq_len` might vary in general, but the reference always uses 32. However, `ModelNew` should accept the same inputs as `Model`, so we should probably handle variable seq_len. But for performance, we can specialize or just use generic code since 32 is small. Having each thread handle one row means we need `seq_len` threads per block. For seq_len=32, we could use one block of 32 threads per (batch, head). But 32 threads is very few. CUDA warp size is 32, so that's exactly one warp. With 16384 warps, we'd have 16384 blocks of 32 threads. This is actually fine! Each warp executes independently. But the memory access pattern: each thread reads an entire row of Q, K, and V. Without shared memory, each K/V element is read `seq_len` times (once per thread in the block). With 32 threads per block, each K/V row is read 32 times. For seq_len=32, there are 32 key rows, each read 32 times = 1024 reads from global memory per block. With shared memory: - Load all K and V into shared memory at the start of the block - Then each thread reads from shared memory instead of global - K: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats, read once from global - V: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats, read once from global - Total global reads for K,V: 4096 floats per block - Without shared memory: 32 threads * 32 cols * 64 dims = 65536 reads - That's a 16x reduction! But shared memory access is slower than register access. We could also load Q into shared memory or registers. Since each thread needs only one row of Q (64 floats), that's small enough for registers. Let me revise with shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel(...) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int row = threadIdx.x; // Load K and V into shared memory // Each thread loads part of K and V for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < seq_len * head_dim; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem[i] = k[batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + i]; v_smem[i] = v[batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Load Q row into registers float q_reg[64]; const float* q_ptr = q + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_reg[d] = q_ptr[d]; } // Compute scores float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e9f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { if (col > row) { scores[col] = -1e9f; } else { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_reg[d] * k_smem[col * head_dim + d]; } scores[col] = dot * rsqrtf((float)head_dim); max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[col]); } } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] = expf(scores[col] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[col]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_reg[64]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { val += scores[col] * v_smem[col * head_dim + d]; } out_reg[d] = val; } // Write output float* out_ptr = out + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[d] = out_reg[d]; } } ``` For the kernel launch: - Grid: batch_size * num_heads blocks - Block: seq_len threads - Shared memory: 2 * seq_len * head_dim * sizeof(float) With seq_len=32, head_dim=64: - Block size: 32 threads - Shared memory: 2 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 16KB - Grid: 16384 blocks Wait, 16KB shared memory per block, and H100 has 227KB shared memory per SM, so we can fit 14 blocks per SM. With 132 SMs, active blocks = 132 * 14 = 1848. Total blocks 16384, so ~8.9 waves. This should be fine but could we do better? If we increase block size to, say, 128 threads, we can load shared memory faster and each block still handles one (batch, head). But the actual computation per thread doesn't change. With larger blocks, the `for` loop for loading shared memory is more parallel, but since we only have 2048 elements to load, 32 threads is already enough (64 loads per thread). So increasing block size doesn't help much. Alternatively, if we process multiple rows per block. Say we use 256 threads, and each block handles 8 rows (8 warps, each warp handles one row). Then: - Block size: 256 threads - Shared memory: still 16KB (all rows share K and V) - Grid: 16384 / 8 = 2048 blocks - Active blocks per SM: 14 - Total active: 1848 - Waves: 2048 / 1848 = 1.1 This is better because we reduce the number of blocks and increase parallelism within each block. But does it matter? The main bottleneck is memory bandwidth and computation within each warp. With 8 warps per block, we have: - 8 warps computing independently - Shared memory is shared, so no bank conflicts if accesses are uniform or properly strided - Each warp reads different Q rows but same K and V from shared memory Actually, if 8 warps are reading K/V from shared memory simultaneously but at different offsets, there could be bank conflicts if the head_dim access pattern causes conflicts. With head_dim=64, and each warp reading one row (64 consecutive floats), if multiple warps read different rows simultaneously, they access different addresses, so no conflicts (each warp reads a different bank range or the same bank range but serialized by the memory controller? Actually, shared memory has 32 banks, and each bank can handle one request per cycle. If 8 warps each request 64 consecutive floats, that's 2 requests per warp per cycle per row. The requests from different warps for different rows will likely map to different banks, so minimal conflicts. I think either approach works fine. Let me go with 32 threads per block (1 warp per block) for simplicity, since it matches seq_len=32 exactly. Wait, actually one issue: with only 32 threads per block, the occupancy might be limited by the warp scheduler, not shared memory or registers. On H100, each SM can have up to 64 warps (2048 threads). With 32 threads per block, max 64 blocks per SM. But shared memory limits us to 14 blocks. So occupancy is ~14/64 = 22%. Not great but probably acceptable. If we use 8 warps per block (256 threads), shared memory still limits to 14 blocks per SM, but now 14 blocks * 256 threads = 3584 threads per SM. Max is 2048 threads, so we'd be limited to 8 blocks per SM. Occupancy = 8 * 256 / 2048 = 100%. That's much better! Let me recalculate: with 16KB shared memory and 227KB total, we can fit floor(227/16) = 14 blocks per SM. With 256 threads each, that's 3584 threads, but max is 2048, so we're limited to 2048/256 = 8 blocks per SM. 8 * 16KB = 128KB < 227KB. So max 8 blocks per SM. Total blocks = 16384 / 8 = 2048 blocks (if each block handles 8 rows). Active blocks at once = 132 * 8 = 1056. Waves = 2048 / 1056 = 1.94. This is quite good. Let me design for this: - Each block handles 8 rows of a single (batch, head) - Block size: 256 threads (8 warps of 32 threads each) - Warp i handles row (i % 8) within the block, but since all warps handle the same block of 8 rows, warp 0 handles row 0, warp 1 handles row 1, etc. - Actually, with 32 threads per warp and 8 warps, each thread in a warp computes one element? No, for our algorithm, each warp handles one row with each thread computing the entire row's attention. Wait, with 32 threads per warp and 64 head_dim: - If each warp handles one row (32 elements in scores, 64 elements in output) - 8 warps handle 8 rows - Total rows per block: 8 - Since seq_len = 32, we'd need 32/8 = 4 such blocks to handle all rows of one (batch, head) Ah, I see the confusion. Each row of the attention corresponds to one query position. There are `seq_len` rows per (batch, head). If each block handles 8 rows, we need `seq_len / 8 = 4` blocks per (batch, head). Let me now write the C++ code. For the attention kernel, I need to handle a tensor of shape (batch, seq_len, num_heads * head_dim) and reshape to (batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim). In PyTorch, after the QKV linear: - q has shape (batch, seq, hidden) - We reshape to (batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim) then permute to (batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim) For the custom kernel, I can take the reshaped/permuted tensor directly. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int row = tid; // Each thread handles one row // Load K and V into shared memory int total_kv = seq_len * head_dim; for (int i = tid; i < total_kv; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem[i] = k[batch_head * total_kv + i]; v_smem[i] = v[batch_head * total_kv + i]; } __syncthreads(); if (row >= seq_len) return; // Load Q row into registers float q_reg[64]; const float* q_ptr = q + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_reg[d] = q_ptr[d]; } // Compute attention scores float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e9f; float scale = rsqrtf((float)head_dim); #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { if (col > row) { scores[col] = -1e9f; } else { float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_reg[d] * k_ptr[d]; } scores[col] = dot * scale; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[col]); } } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] = expf(scores[col] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[col]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { scores[col] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float* out_ptr = out + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { val += scores[col] * v_smem[col * head_dim + d]; } out_ptr[d] = val; } } ``` This has a few hardcoded constants (64 for head_dim, 32 for seq_len). Since head_dim = 1024/16 = 64, and seq_len is typically 32 for this problem, these happen to match. But for a more general kernel, I should use parameters. For performance, `#pragma unroll` is crucial for small loops. Since head_dim=64 and seq_len=32 are powers of 2, the compiler should unroll nicely if the loops have constant bounds. But if the function takes `seq_len` and `head_dim` as variables, the compiler might not know they're constants. I can address this with templates or by using constants. Actually, I can write the kernel with template parameters: ```cuda template <int SeqLen, int HeadDim> __global__ void attention_kernel(...) { ... } ``` Then dispatch based on the actual values: ```cpp if (seq_len == 32 && head_dim == 64) { attention_kernel<32, 64><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); } ``` This ensures the compiler can fully unroll all loops. Let me check: BART-large has hidden_size=1024, num_heads=16, so head_dim=64. The sequence length is 32. But could it vary? The `forward()` should be correct for any input shape. I should either template for common shapes or write a generic version. I'll write a generic version first, then add templated specializations for the common case (seq_len=32, head_dim=64). Actually, I can just write the generic version with `#pragma unroll` and trust that the compiler handles small constant-folded loops reasonably well. But explicit templates are safer for performance. Wait, there's another optimization. I can use `float4` or vectorized loads/stores. For head_dim=64, each row has 64 floats = 16 float4s. This can improve memory bandwidth. Let me revise the kernel with float4: ```cuda template <int SeqLen, int HeadDim> __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + SeqLen * HeadDim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load K and V into shared memory using float4 int total_kv = SeqLen * HeadDim; int vec_size = 4; int total_vec = total_kv / vec_size; const float4* k_vec = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k + batch_head * total_kv); const float4* v_vec = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v + batch_head * total_kv); float4* k_smem_vec = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(k_smem); float4* v_smem_vec = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(v_smem); for (int i = tid; i < total_vec; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem_vec[i] = k_vec[i]; v_smem_vec[i] = v_vec[i]; } __syncthreads(); int row = tid; if (row >= SeqLen) return; // Load Q row float q_reg[HeadDim]; const float* q_ptr = q + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim + row * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { float4 q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[d/4]; q_reg[d] = q4.x; q_reg[d+1] = q4.y; q_reg[d+2] = q4.z; q_reg[d+3] = q4.w; } // Compute scores float scores[SeqLen]; float max_score = -1e9f; const float scale = rsqrtf((float)HeadDim); #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { if (col > row) { scores[col] = -1e9f; } else { float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { dot += q_reg[d] * k_ptr[d]; dot += q_reg[d+1] * k_ptr[d+1]; dot += q_reg[d+2] * k_ptr[d+2]; dot += q_reg[d+3] * k_ptr[d+3]; } scores[col] = dot * scale; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[col]); } } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { scores[col] = expf(scores[col] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[col]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_sum; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { scores[col] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_reg[HeadDim]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d++) { out_reg[d] = 0.0f; } #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { const float* v_ptr = v_smem + col * HeadDim; float s = scores[col]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { out_reg[d] += s * v_ptr[d]; out_reg[d+1] += s * v_ptr[d+1]; out_reg[d+2] += s * v_ptr[d+2]; out_reg[d+3] += s * v_ptr[d+3]; } } // Write output with float4 float* out_ptr = out + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim + row * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { float4 o4; o4.x = out_reg[d]; o4.y = out_reg[d+1]; o4.z = out_reg[d+2]; o4.w = out_reg[d+3]; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(out_ptr)[d/4] = o4; } } ``` This is more complex but should be faster. However, `float4` requires 16-byte alignment. For head_dim=64 and seq_len=32, the total size of each (batch, head) block is 32 * 64 = 2048 floats = 8192 bytes. Since we allocate contiguous memory for all batch heads, each block starts at an offset of 8192 bytes from the previous, which is divisible by 16. So alignment should be fine. For the block size, with `HeadDim=64` and `SeqLen=32`, we need 32 threads. I could use more threads to help load shared memory, but with float4 loads, 32 threads only need 2048/4/32 = 16 iterations each to load K and V. That's very fast. Wait, the total shared memory is 2 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 16384 bytes. That's reasonable. Now for the C++ host code. I need to handle: 1. Input embedding 2. Positional embedding 3. LayerNorm embedding 4. Loop over 12 layers 5. For each layer: self-attention layer norm, QKV linears, custom attention, output linear, residual 6. FFN layer norm, fc1 linear, GELU, fc2 linear, residual 7. Final step: LM head In BART decoder, there actually isn't a final layer norm after all decoder layers. The hidden states from the last layer go directly to the LM head. Wait, let me verify. In BartDecoder: - After the loop through layers, it returns the hidden states - There's no post-decoder LayerNorm in standard BartDecoder Actually, in `modeling_bart.py`, looking at the `BartDecoder` class, after the layer loop: ```python # add hidden states from the last decoder layer if output_hidden_states: all_hidden_states += (hidden_states,) next_cache = next_decoder_cache if use_cache else None if not return_dict: return tuple(v for v in [hidden_states, next_cache, all_hidden_states, all_self_attns, all_cross_attentions] if v is not None) return BaseModelOutputWithPastAndCrossAttentions(...) ``` So no final layer norm. The hidden states go directly to `lm_head`. For `BartForCausalLM`: ```python logits = self.lm_head(outputs[0]) ``` So the forward is: ``` embed -> decoder layers -> lm_head ``` With no final norm. Good. Now, positional embeddings in BART. The `BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding` takes input_ids and computes positions as: ```python # create positions positions = torch.arange( past_key_values_length, past_key_values_length + seq_len, dtype=torch.long, device=input_ids.device ) positions = positions.unsqueeze(0).expand(input_ids.shape[0], -1) ``` For causal LM without past_key_values, `past_key_values_length = 0`, so positions are `arange(0, seq_len)`. Wait, in BartDecoder: ```python # embed positions positions = self.embed_positions(input, past_key_values_length) ``` And `BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding.forward`: ```python def forward(self, input_ids: torch.Tensor, past_key_values_length: int = 0): bsz, seq_len = input_ids.shape[:2] positions = torch.arange( past_key_values_length, past_key_values_length + seq_len, dtype=torch.long, device=input_ids.device ) positions = positions.unsqueeze(0).expand(bsz, -1) return super().forward(positions) ``` And the embedding layer has `offset=2`, so positions + 2 are used as indices. Let me double-check. In `BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding.__init__`: ```python self.offset = 2 self.weight = nn.Embedding(num_embeddings + self.offset, embedding_dim) ``` And `forward` calls `super().forward(positions + self.offset)`. Yes, so positions go from 0 to seq_len-1, and we add `offset=2` to get indices from 2 to seq_len+1. In my C++ code: ```cpp auto positions = at::arange(seq_len, at::kLong, input_ids.device()) + 2; positions = positions.unsqueeze(0).expand({batch_size, -1}); auto pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions, positions); ``` For the embedding of tokens, BART does: ```python inputs_embeds = self.embed_tokens(input_ids) * self.embed_scale ``` where `embed_scale = sqrt(hidden_size)` typically. Wait, in `BartDecoder.__init__`: ```python self.embed_scale = math.sqrt(embed_dim) if scale_embedding else 1.0 ``` And for `bart-large`, `scale_embedding` is typically True, so `embed_scale = sqrt(1024) = 32.0`. Let me extract and use this. Also, in `BartDecoder`: ```python hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds hidden_states = self.layernorm_embedding(hidden_states) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) ``` Dropout is skipped in eval mode. Now for the decoder layer (BartDecoderLayer): ```python residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) # Self Attention hidden_states, _, _ = self.self_attn( hidden_states=hidden_states, ... ) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states # Cross-Attention (skipped if no encoder_hidden_states) if encoder_hidden_states is not None: ... # Fully Connected residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.activation_fn(self.fc1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.activation_dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = self.fc2(hidden_states) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states ``` Since we're in eval mode and no encoder_hidden_states, cross-attention is skipped. For activation, BART uses `config.activation_function` which defaults to `"gelu"`. GELU in HF BART is typically `nn.functional.gelu`. One more thing: the self-attention in Bart uses `need_weights=False` for training, but for generation/inference it might compute weights. In our case, we don't need weights. Also, the causal mask in BART decoder. Looking at `BartDecoder._prepare_decoder_attention_mask`: ```python combined_attention_mask = _make_causal_mask( input_shape, inputs_embeds.dtype, device=inputs_embeds.device, past_key_values_length=past_key_values_length, ) ``` `_make_causal_mask` creates a mask of shape `(batch, 1, seq, seq)` with `-inf` for future positions and 0 for past positions. In the attention computation, this mask is added to the attention scores: ```python attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask ``` And we use `1e-9` or `-inf` for the masked positions. In our custom kernel, we hardcode the causal mask. Wait, I should also check if the attention needs attention_mask for padding. For `input_ids`, if all tokens are valid (no padding), then only the causal mask is needed. The inputs from `get_inputs()` are just random integers, so no padding mask is needed. Good, so we only need the causal mask in our attention kernel. Now let me think about whether to write the entire forward in C++ or in Python. Actually, writing it in Python is much simpler for extracting weights and debugging. The main overhead of Python for a 12-layer loop is minimal. Let me write it in Python first, with the custom attention kernel, and see if it works. If the speedup isn't enough, I can move more to C++. Actually, I'll write a Python class that extracts weights and uses PyTorch ops, with a custom CUDA kernel for attention. This is the most practical approach. Wait, but then I need to write the CUDA kernel with `load_inline`. For the kernel, I just need one function: ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) ``` This function: 1. Gets shapes from tensors 2. Creates output tensor 3. Launches the attention kernel 4. Returns output And I can write a template dispatch: ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int num_heads = q.size(1); int seq_len = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); auto out = at::empty_like(q); int grid = batch_size * num_heads; int block = seq_len; size_t shared_mem = 2 * seq_len * head_dim * sizeof(float); if (seq_len == 32 && head_dim == 64) { attention_kernel<32, 64><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads ); } else { attention_kernel_generic<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim ); } return out; } ``` Wait, CUDA tensors might be on different devices. `load_inline` handles this if we pass tensors from Python. One important thing: the tensors Q, K, V need to be contiguous and of type float32. Also, I should use `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES` for type dispatch, but since the problem specifies fp32, I can just use `float`. Actually, pybind11 with torch tensors handles types automatically. Using bf16/fp16 internally for GEMMs might still meet the tolerance if the final output is cast back to fp32. But this adds complexity and might cause numerical issues. For simplicity, I'll stick to fp32 throughout. Now let me draft the full Python code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Define the CUDA source cuda_source = ''' // ... attention kernel ... torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // ... implementation ... } ''' cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> // Declare the CUDA kernel launcher or put it all in cuda_sources torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int num_heads = q.size(1); int seq_len = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); auto out = at::empty_like(q); dim3 grid(batch_size * num_heads); dim3 block(seq_len); size_t shared_mem = 2 * seq_len * head_dim * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(q.scalar_type(), "bart_attention_forward", [&] { // For now, just use float specialization }); // Actually, we need CUDA kernel declaration here. Since we're using load_inline with cpp_sources and cuda_sources, // the CUDA kernel goes in cuda_sources and we declare it in cpp_sources or put it all in one source. return out; } ''' ``` Wait, `load_inline` takes both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. The CUDA kernels go in `cuda_sources`, and the C++ wrapper that calls them goes in `cpp_sources`. Actually, for simplicity, I can put the CUDA kernel in `cuda_sources` and declare it as `extern` in `cpp_sources`, or I can put everything in `cuda_sources` if it's all CUDA code. But the host code that creates tensors and launches kernels must be in C++ (`.cpp` or `.cu`). But `load_inline` separates them. Hmm, let me look up typical usage of `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`: ```python my_extension = load_inline( name="my_extension", cpp_sources="...", cuda_sources="...", functions=["my_function"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--use_fast_math"], ) ``` If I put the kernel in `cuda_sources` and the host code in `cpp_sources`, I need to forward-declare the kernel in `cpp_sources` or somehow make it visible. Actually, the NVCC compiler compiles `.cu` files and generates host stubs for `__global__` functions. These stubs have C++ linkage. If I declare the kernel as `void my_kernel(...)` in the `cpp_sources`, the linker should find it from the `cuda_sources` object file. Let me try this approach: **cuda_sources**: ```cuda template <int SeqLen, int HeadDim> __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads ) { ... } ``` **cpp_sources**: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> template <int SeqLen, int HeadDim> __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads ); torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // ... launch kernel ... attention_kernel<32, 64><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); return out; } ``` Actually, you CANNOT forward-declare a template `__global__` function like that because the template instantiation is done in the `.cu` file, and the host stub is generated there. But you can forward-declare the instantiated function: ```cpp __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads ); ``` Wait, template instantiations are just regular functions with mangled names. Forward declaring them is tricky. A better approach: 1. In `cuda_sources`, define the template and explicit instantiations: ```cuda template <int SeqLen, int HeadDim> __global__ void attention_kernel(...) { ... } // Explicit instantiation template __global__ void attention_kernel<32, 64>( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads ); ``` 2. In `cpp_sources`, forward declare the instantiated function, or just declare a non-template wrapper kernel that calls the template internally. Actually, an easier approach: define a non-template wrapper kernel in `cuda_sources` that calls the template internally or just contains the logic with runtime parameters. Or even simpler: write a generic kernel without templates, using runtime parameters. Since seq_len is small (32), and the compiler might not unroll perfectly, but it should still be OK. For seq_len=32 and head_dim=64, we're reading/writing memory more than computing, so loop unrolling isn't the difference between fast and slow. Memory coalescing is more important. Let me just write a generic kernel. It'll be simpler and less error-prone. Actually, you know what, let me just put everything in `cuda_sources` and define the host function there too. The `cpp_sources` can be empty or just minimal. NVCC can compile host C++ code in `.cu` files just fine. Wait, but `load_inline` might expect `cpp_sources` to contain the functions listed in `functions=[...]`. Let me check: the `functions` parameter tells pybind11 which functions to expose. These functions can be in either `cpp_sources` or `cuda_sources`, as long as they have C++ linkage. Actually, I think they need to be in `cpp_sources` because pybind11 processes the C++ code. But NVCC can handle everything. Let me try a different structure: use `cpp_sources` for all the host code and pybind11-exposed functions, and `cuda_sources` for just the `__global__` kernels. Then forward-declare the kernels in `cpp_sources` as non-template functions or use a wrapper. To avoid template instantiation issues, I'll define a non-template kernel in `cuda_sources`: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load K and V int total_kv = seq_len * head_dim; for (int i = tid; i < total_kv; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem[i] = k[batch_head * total_kv + i]; v_smem[i] = v[batch_head * total_kv + i]; } __syncthreads(); int row = tid; if (row >= seq_len) return; // Load Q // ... but head_dim is dynamic, so we can't use fixed-size arrays } ``` With dynamic `head_dim`, I can't easily use registers for `q_reg` and `out_reg`. I'd need to use shared memory or local memory (spilled to global memory). For seq_len=32 and head_dim=64, I know these values at compile time for the common case. So templates are really the way to go. Alternative: dispatch to different kernels based on head_dim. Since BART always has head_dim=64 for these parameters, I can just write a specialized kernel for 64. Let me do this: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel_32_64( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads ) { const int SeqLen = 32; const int HeadDim = 64; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + SeqLen * HeadDim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Vectorized load const float4* k_vec = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim); const float4* v_vec = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim); float4* k_smem_vec = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(k_smem); float4* v_smem_vec = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(v_smem); int total_vec = SeqLen * HeadDim / 4; for (int i = tid; i < total_vec; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem_vec[i] = k_vec[i]; v_smem_vec[i] = v_vec[i]; } __syncthreads(); int row = tid; if (row >= SeqLen) return; // Load Q row into registers float q_reg[HeadDim]; const float* q_ptr = q + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim + row * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { float4 q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[d/4]; q_reg[d] = q4.x; q_reg[d+1] = q4.y; q_reg[d+2] = q4.z; q_reg[d+3] = q4.w; } // Compute scores float scores[SeqLen]; float max_score = -1e9f; const float scale = rsqrtf((float)HeadDim); #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { if (col > row) { scores[col] = -1e9f; } else { float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { dot += q_reg[d] * k_ptr[d]; dot += q_reg[d+1] * k_ptr[d+1]; dot += q_reg[d+2] * k_ptr[d+2]; dot += q_reg[d+3] * k_ptr[d+3]; } scores[col] = dot * scale; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[col]); } } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { scores[col] = expf(scores[col] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[col]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { scores[col] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_reg[HeadDim]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d++) { out_reg[d] = 0.0f; } #pragma unroll for (int col = 0; col < SeqLen; col++) { const float* v_ptr = v_smem + col * HeadDim; float s = scores[col]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { out_reg[d] += s * v_ptr[d]; out_reg[d+1] += s * v_ptr[d+1]; out_reg[d+2] += s * v_ptr[d+2]; out_reg[d+3] += s * v_ptr[d+3]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = out + batch_head * SeqLen * HeadDim + row * HeadDim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HeadDim; d += 4) { float4 o4; o4.x = out_reg[d]; o4.y = out_reg[d+1]; o4.z = out_reg[d+2]; o4.w = out_reg[d+3]; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(out_ptr)[d/4] = o4; } } ``` And a generic fallback kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel_generic(...) { ... } ``` Then in C++: ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int num_heads = q.size(1); int seq_len = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); auto out = at::empty_like(q); int grid = batch_size * num_heads; int block = seq_len; size_t shared_mem = 2 * seq_len * head_dim * sizeof(float); if (seq_len == 32 && head_dim == 64) { attention_kernel_32_64<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads ); } else { attention_kernel_generic<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim ); } return out; } ``` For the generic kernel, I can use shared memory for Q row, scores, etc. Or just keep it simple since it's a fallback. Actually, for the generic kernel, I'll just use shared memory for K and V, and local memory for Q row. But local memory for a dynamic array size will spill. For a fallback that's rarely used, it's fine. But to avoid compilation issues, I can just write a generic version with fixed maximum sizes: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel_generic( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; int batch_head = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int total_kv = seq_len * head_dim; for (int i = tid; i < total_kv; i += blockDim.x) { k_smem[i] = k[batch_head * total_kv + i]; v_smem[i] = v[batch_head * total_kv + i]; } __syncthreads(); int row = tid; if (row >= seq_len) return; const float* q_ptr = q + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; float* out_ptr = out + batch_head * seq_len * head_dim + row * head_dim; // Compute scores in local memory (spill is OK for fallback) float max_score = -1e9f; float scale = rsqrtf((float)head_dim); for (int col = 0; col < seq_len; col++) { if (col > row) continue; float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_ptr[d] * k_ptr[d]; } max_score = fmaxf(max_score, dot * scale); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int col = 0; col <= row; col++) { float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_ptr[d] * k_ptr[d]; } float score = expf(dot * scale - max_score); sum_exp += score; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float val = 0.0f; for (int col = 0; col <= row; col++) { float dot = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k_smem + col * head_dim; for (int dd = 0; dd < head_dim; dd++) { dot += q_ptr[dd] * k_ptr[dd]; } float score = expf(dot * scale - max_score) * inv_sum; val += score * v_smem[col * head_dim + d]; } out_ptr[d] = val; } } ``` Wait, this recomputes dot products multiple times which is inefficient. For a fallback, I can just precompute all scores and store them: ```cuda // After the __shared__ declarations float scores[128]; // assume max seq_len ``` But dynamic seq_len might exceed 128. I'll use a conservative approach and just compute as needed. Since it's a fallback and likely won't be used for the benchmark, performance doesn't matter much. Actually, to keep the code clean, let me just require that the kernel supports any seq_len and head_dim by using a simple approach with local memory arrays sized to the problem. But CUDA doesn't support VLAs. I'll just recompute. Wait, actually there's a simpler approach: just call PyTorch's `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` or similar from C++ if the shapes don't match our optimized kernel! In `bart_attention_forward`, if seq_len != 32 or head_dim != 64, I can just call `at::native::scaled_dot_product_attention` or manually compute with PyTorch ops. Actually, looking at PyTorch's API, `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` is available. I can use that as fallback! ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int seq_len = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); if (seq_len == 32 && head_dim == 64) { // custom kernel return custom_attention(q, k, v); } else { // PyTorch fallback auto scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) / sqrt(head_dim); auto causal_mask = at::triu(at::full({seq_len, seq_len}, -1e9, q.options()), 1); scores = scores + causal_mask; auto attn = at::softmax(scores, -1); return at::matmul(attn, v); } } ``` This is perfect! For the common case (32, 64), we use the fast custom kernel. For any other shape, we use the correct PyTorch fallback. Now let me think about whether to write the rest of the model in Python or C++. If I write it in Python, the code would be: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) ref_model.eval() decoder = ref_model.model.decoder # Extract embeddings self.embed_tokens = decoder.embed_tokens.weight.detach() self.embed_positions = decoder.embed_positions.weight.detach() self.layernorm_embedding_weight = decoder.layernorm_embedding.weight.detach() self.layernorm_embedding_bias = decoder.layernorm_embedding.bias.detach() # Determine embed_scale self.embed_scale = decoder.embed_scale # Extract layer weights self.num_layers = len(decoder.layers) self.self_attn_layer_norm_weights = [] self.self_attn_layer_norm_biases = [] # ... etc for layer in decoder.layers: self.self_attn_layer_norm_weights.append(layer.self_attn_layer_norm.weight.detach()) # ... etc for all weights self.self_attn_layer_norm_weights = torch.stack(self.self_attn_layer_norm_weights) # ... etc self.lm_head = ref_model.lm_head.weight.detach() # Compile CUDA extension self.cuda_module = load_inline( name="bart_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["bart_attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) def forward(self, x): batch_size, seq_len = x.shape # Embedding hidden = F.embedding(x, self.embed_tokens) * self.embed_scale positions = torch.arange(2, seq_len + 2, device=x.device, dtype=torch.long) positions = positions.unsqueeze(0).expand(batch_size, -1) pos_embeds = F.embedding(positions, self.embed_positions) hidden = hidden + pos_embeds hidden = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.layernorm_embedding_weight, self.layernorm_embedding_bias, 1e-5) # Decoder layers for i in range(self.num_layers): residual = hidden hidden = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.self_attn_layer_norm_weights[i], self.self_attn_layer_norm_biases[i], 1e-5) q = F.linear(hidden, self.q_weights[i], self.q_biases[i]) k = F.linear(hidden, self.k_weights[i], self.k_biases[i]) v = F.linear(hidden, self.v_weights[i], self.v_biases[i]) # Reshape for multi-head attention q = q.view(batch_size, seq_len, 16, 64).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(batch_size, seq_len, 16, 64).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(batch_size, seq_len, 16, 64).transpose(1, 2) attn_out = self.cuda_module.bart_attention_forward(q, k, v) attn_out = attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch_size, seq_len, 1024) hidden = F.linear(attn_out, self.out_proj_weights[i], self.out_proj_biases[i]) hidden = residual + hidden # FFN residual = hidden hidden = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.final_layer_norm_weights[i], self.final_layer_norm_biases[i], 1e-5) hidden = F.linear(hidden, self.fc1_weights[i], self.fc1_biases[i]) hidden = F.gelu(hidden) hidden = F.linear(hidden, self.fc2_weights[i], self.fc2_biases[i]) hidden = residual + hidden # LM head logits = F.linear(hidden, self.lm_head) return logits ``` This looks good. Let me carefully verify all the operations match HuggingFace's implementation. One thing: in BART, the self-attention has a `out_proj` which is `nn.Linear(hidden_size, hidden_size, bias=True)`. In our code, we use `F.linear(attn_out, self.out_proj_weights[i], self.out_proj_biases[i])`. The weight shape for `nn.Linear` is `(out_features, in_features)`, so `out_proj.weight` has shape `(1024, 1024)`. `F.linear(input, weight, bias)` computes `input @ weight.T + bias`. This is correct. For Q, K, V projections: `q_proj.weight` is `(1024, 1024)`, `q_proj.bias` is `(1024,)`. `F.linear(hidden, q_weight, q_bias)` computes `hidden @ q_weight.T + q_bias`. This matches. For the FFN: `fc1.weight` is `(4096, 1024)`, `fc2.weight` is `(1024, 4096)`. Correct. For the LM head: `lm_head.weight` is `(50265, 1024)`. Correct. Let me now think about potential correctness issues: 1. **Causal mask**: In our custom kernel, we set future positions to `-inf` using `if (col > row)`. The exact implementation might use `torch.finfo(dtype).min` which is about `-3.4e38` for float32. In our kernel, we use `-1e9f`. `-1e9` is not the same as `-3.4e38`. Will this affect softmax? In softmax, if one value is -1e9 and others are normal (e.g., ~0 to ~10), then exp(-1e9) is effectively 0, and exp(0) is 1. The result is the same as if it were -inf: the masked positions get probability 0. But what if the unmasked scores are also negative and around -1e9? Then `-1e9` might not be a strong enough mask. However, for attention scores in a properly scaled model (scale = sqrt(64) = 8), dot products are typically in the range of a few to tens. So -1e9 is plenty. Actually, let me use a larger negative number to be safe, like `-1e9` or `FLT_MIN/2`. But `expf(-1e9)` underflows to 0 in float32, which is fine. Let me stick with `-1e9f`. Hmm, but in PyTorch, the mask is added to the attention scores: ```python attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask ``` Where `attention_mask` contains 0 for attended positions and a large negative for masked positions. In our kernel, we directly set masked positions to a large negative before softmax. This is equivalent. 2. **Softmax numerical stability**: We subtract `max_score` before exponentiating. This is correct and standard. 3. **Scale factor**: We use `1/sqrt(head_dim)` which is correct for BART. 4. **LayerNorm epsilon**: BART uses `1e-5` for layer norm.
